To sum up: if you see an article that both matches what you already believe and falls into the "whoa, if true" category? Don't tweet it out until you've read it very, very carefully and you're sure you're not converting "maybe" into "definitely" in your head. 8/end
Why not? Because "messy data exists" wouldn't confirm @JohnCornyn's belief that noncitizens successfully vote. But a headline implying that noncitizens did vote? That does. It's very easy - and we all do this sometimes - to go from "maybe" to "definitely" in case like this. 7/n
In other words, this article could have just as easily been headlined "22 years of changing voter registration requirements result in messy data, officials say". That would have been just as accurate, although it probably wouldn't have garnered a link from the Senator. 6/n

@untoldbumpkins It doesn't ignore the search text. It's just not ordering by the search text.  And it depends on what you're searching for.
@untoldbumpkins Because I have other things I need to do besides answering your questions, I answer as many as I can get to at a time. If you want to send them all in an email, that'd be easier.
@untoldbumpkins I have a long list of many other things that need fixing on GovTrack, two other jobs, and other life responsibilities. It'll get fixed when it gets fixed, unfortunately. (Also I have no idea what you think the fix is supposed to be that wouldn't make it worse for other uses.)
@untoldbumpkins Here's our code repository: https://t.co/E1ahSiQBpb If it's so easy, please go ahead and make the change!
@untoldbumpkins And I'm not saying that's intuitive. It's not. I'm just letting you know how the site works today.
@untoldbumpkins There's a different default depending on how you get to the search page. If you start with a search in the search bar at the top of every page, it defaults to Relevance. Elsewhere to secret sauce. Depending on what you're looking for, that can be helpful.
@Moore1766 I don't think we've ever applied for a grant from the Open Society Foundation, no. I have done consulting work for them, so I guess the answer is yes once? ~JT
@untoldbumpkins That's me. (I'm not the only person who operates our twitter account, but I am now.)  I wrote the "secret sauce" but not the "Relevance" algorithm.
@untoldbumpkins The last thing to keep in mind is there's no bill currently before Congress with that title. Our search results all default to showing bills currently pending before Congress because that's the primary way we believe people are intending to use the site. It's not always ideal.
@StanleyNormoyle That has nothing to do with whether we're corporate or not.....
@untoldbumpkins The "Relevnace" sort order means relevance of the bill title and text to your keyword search, so that's what you want to use. It's the Solr search engine default sort order.
@untoldbumpkins The secret sauce isn't really a secret. It's the result of a linear regression that takes into account various factors correlated with bills being enacted. It doesn't take into account your keyword search term, which is why it doesn't help you find that bill...

So which law applies? The Vacancies Act's committee report specifically says that 28 USC 508 overrules the Vacancies Act: "Senate confirmed Justice Department officials will be the only persons eligible to serve as Acting Attorney General." https://t.co/ihTtH6b39K (5/5)
An earlier law 28 USC 508 specifically addressed how to fill the Attorney General's position. "In case of a vacancy in the office of Attorney General...the Deputy Attorney General may exercise all the duties of that office." https://t.co/0fBD36nEXW (4/n)
The Vacancies Act governs most cases of temporarily filling a vacancy of a Senate-confirmed position, which (generally speaking) allows someone in Whitaker's position to step in. Here's a good overview of that law: https://t.co/WJ8dvDGmS7 But it might not apply for the AG! (3/n)
.@lawfareblog gives a good overview of the Constitutional (the Senate must give advice &amp; consent for the position), statutory (there are laws specifically about this), and recusal issues: https://t.co/3DiHdzzbcq (2/n)
There's some serious legal debate going on about whether President Trump's appointment of Sessions's former chief of staff to be acting Attorney General complies with both the Constitution and long-standing statutes about filling vacancies. (1/n)

This puts the US in a situation not envisioned by the founders and it's not unreasonable to look at the effects of this structure and ask ourselves "is this still what we want"? /end
Over time, political parties have solidified into the current 2-party system &amp; less populous states are much more likely to elect GOP Senators. This confers the increasing power of less populous states onto a single political party. +
Also, when the Constitution was drafted and passed, there were no national political parties, so the discussion was exclusively about whether and how to ensure less populous states didn't get railroaded by more populous ones. + https://t.co/zEgsjKZbml
The Senate's structure was designed to strengthen the voice of _numerical_ minorities and it works. Since its founding, the nation's population has become much less equally distributed with CA, TX, FL and NY as significant outliers. + https://t.co/xNV9VUsUk8
Looking over responses to our Senate representation article this morning, it seems like there's some conflation of political minorities, numerical minorities and which the Senate structure was intended to protect. +
@Kalani_Reed We didn't say the majority of people support any particular issue that was voted on. Since we looked at hundreds of thousands of votes, there's no way for us to know that.

There are many partisan ways to participate in elections, but working at polls is uniquely rewarding because it's solely in service to the democratic process w/o regard to parties. If your work/schedule allows, you should do it too!  /end
Lots of parents brought their kids (often as a civics lesson if they were old enough) &amp; if you're the person handing out the I Voted stickers, woo, are you ever popular. This was a complete and delightful surprise.
After all, you don't just learn how the sausage is made, you're there helping to make it. Were the machines ready to go and properly set to the day, time &amp; zero count? Yes. You verified it. Were the paper ballots properly secured? Yes. You secured them. Etc etc.
It's the morning after the Florida primaries in which your GovTracker was a poll worker yesterday &amp; I'm here to evangelize some more. It's a long, but fun and satisfying way to spend a day that also reinforces your confidence in the electoral process.

@Doverpost Sounds like it, but then another way to describe ad hominem is â€œconsider the sourceâ€. Not sure if it says more about logic or people that perfectly good advice for social interactions is also often logically fallacious.
So, we think it's an appeal to consequences. Rep. Zeldin says that if everyone protested injustice, the end result would be bad because nothing else would get done, so these specific people shouldn't protest it in this circumstance. 5/5
It's also not a slippery slope fallacy because he's not claiming that since NFL players have done this one thing, now everyone else has started to as well. Instead, he speculates on what would happen if everyone did. 4/5
"Moralistic fallacy" sounds a lot like "moralizing", e.g. to make claims about what's right to do or not to do. You could argue that Rep. Zeldin is moralizing about the issue, but that's not a logical fallacy. 3/5
The moralistic fallacy is when you infer what is the case from what ought to be the case.  Clearly, Rep. Zeldin thinks NFL players shouldn't kneel - he says so - but he's not suggesting that they _aren't_ kneeling. 2/5
Belated follow up to this week's Fallacy Friday in which Rep. Zeldin said that if everyone took a knee over a cause, no one would ever be standing so NFL players shouldn't do it during the anthem. 61% said it was the moralistic fallacy. However, that's not it. 1/5
